<div id="review_292660681" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/292660681" />
    <a title="Lisa" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1758411-lisa"><img alt="Lisa" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1229828252p2/1758411.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/292660681?book_show_action=true">Mar 11, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Lisa" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Lisa" href="/user/show/1758411-lisa">Lisa</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1758411-lisa?shelf=kindle">kindle</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1758411-lisa?shelf=japan">japan</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer292660681" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8771069584154152849">Of all the books longlisted for the Man Asian Literary Prize that I’ve read so far, 1Q84 is the most problematic for me. Unlike the rest of the Known World, I’ve never read Murakami before, so I can’t compare this book with his previous work or put it in context with his literary oeuvre. That’s a problem, with an author so well-known…<br/> <br/>On the other hand, I come to the book with no Murakami baggage, and no expectations. And expectations there were aplenty, apparently, and alongside the rave review</span>
  <span id="freeText8771069584154152849" style="display:none">Of all the books longlisted for the Man Asian Literary Prize that I’ve read so far, 1Q84 is the most problematic for me. Unlike the rest of the Known World, I’ve never read Murakami before, so I can’t compare this book with his previous work or put it in context with his literary oeuvre. That’s a problem, with an author so well-known…<br /> <br />On the other hand, I come to the book with no Murakami baggage, and no expectations. And expectations there were aplenty, apparently, and alongside the rave reviews from readers at GoodReads, Amazon and Library Thing, there have been mutterings of dismay. ’2011′s Biggest Literary Letdown’ says one, and you can find a useful summary of the critical reception of this book at Neojapanisme. I read none of these until I had read two-thirds of the book and had more-or-less formed my opinion of it, and I’ve still only scanned a few of them. Murakami criticism is an industry, it appears, and you could read an entire novel in the time it would take to read it all.<br /> <br />So why should I spend my time adding to it, eh? Actually, the plan was to wait-and-see if 1Q84 made the Man Asian shortlist and defer reading it if it didn’t. I wanted to read some of Murakami’s other books first, but after reading The Lake I became curious about contemporary Japanese literature. Did Murakami have anything in common with Yoshimoto? Why are these two authors mega-popular in the West as well as in Japan, when most other translated authors are comprehensively ignored by the English-speaking world? Are Murakami and Yoshimoto Japanese Literature Lite??<br /> <br />It seems to me that they do have themes in common: a sense of regret over wasted opportunities pervades 1Q84. Tengo was a maths prodigy as a child but he rejected the certainties of maths and now he’s a hack writer. Aomame was a promising athlete but now she’s a masseuse (with serial killing as a sideline). These two are thirty, but though they’ve spent 20 years remembering holding hands as school kids, neither has taken the initiative to find the other. Like the characters in The Lake they are profoundly lonely people in one of the most crowded cities in the world: both are wholly estranged from their families and have no friends. (In 1Q84 both the main characters have by necessity an unemotional attitude to sex: they have practical arrangements for getting it when they want to satisfy mere animal appetite, no commitment necessary). What’s more, in both books the central characters are victims of strange upbringing but they end up taking control, which they do with a mixture of intuition – which in a soulless age can apparently be trusted – and a sense of destiny.<br /><br />To read the rest of my review please visit <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://anzlitlovers.com/2011/12/30/1q84-by-haruki-murakami-translated-by-jay-rubin-and-philip-gabriel-shadow-man-asian-literary-prize-2011-2/">http://anzlitlovers.com/2011/12/30/1q...</a></span>
  <a data-text-id="8771069584154152849" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_2359435683" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2359435683" />
    <a title="Marina Morais" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/27281644-marina-morais"><img alt="Marina Morais" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1388748110p2/27281644.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2359435683?book_show_action=true">Apr 19, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Marina Morais" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Marina Morais" href="/user/show/27281644-marina-morais">Marina Morais</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9788579622649" href="/book/show/18780422-1q84---livro-3">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/27281644-marina-morais?shelf=my-books">my-books</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2359435683" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1563612138853563483">I don't even think this book is worse than the first, which I rated 3 stars. But it's the last book of a trilogy that brings more questions than answers, as repeatedly stated throughout all three installments. I haven't felt this disappointed since I finished "A Series of Unfortunate Events". Actually, I wasn't exactly disappointed this time, considering that I had basically lost hope since Book 2...<br/><br/>I don't get it. Murakami is a good writer, but even his writing is irregular in this trilogy. The</span>
  <span id="freeText1563612138853563483" style="display:none">I don't even think this book is worse than the first, which I rated 3 stars. But it's the last book of a trilogy that brings more questions than answers, as repeatedly stated throughout all three installments. I haven't felt this disappointed since I finished "A Series of Unfortunate Events". Actually, I wasn't exactly disappointed this time, considering that I had basically lost hope since Book 2...<br /><br />I don't get it. Murakami is a good writer, but even his writing is irregular in this trilogy. The plot is not particularly creative, several people have created a parallel universe of sorts in their stories. See Philip Pullman. See Murakami himself in "Dance Dance Dance". It's not new, so you have to make it new. The only thing that's new about "1Q84" is that it tries to justify the rape of underage girls with religion and supernatural motives, which, let's face it, is the lamest excuse ever.<br /><br />And all the bed sex... Man, it felt like sex ed material, but in ancient times. And I thought Morrisey didn't have competition!<br /><br />Just like Lemony Snicket in "A Series of Unfortunate Events", Murakami turns an initially simple story into a complex web of characters and events, all related and, at the same time, all connected by chance (or something deeper than chance), just to throw it all away in the end and leave you to make something of it if you want to. Well, I don't want to, thank you very much. I'm just glad it's over.<br /><br />Aomame is a great character, until she's not anymore. The ingenuity of her relationship with Tengo doesn't fit her character at all. Tengo seems to come to the conclusion that he was raped by a 17-year-old girl, so there you have it. I think the only character that remains steady from beginning to end is Tamaru.<br /><br />It's still easy-ish to ready because, well, Murakami. But, please, if you're a Brazilian reader who can read in English, DO NOT READ THE PORTUGUESE TRANSLATION. It's the worst!!! Especially when you're a grammar freak and can't stand the constant mistakes. I just hope it was the publisher's fault, that somehow they forced the translator to forget interpretation and just copy out the author's words from the original language, ignoring the necessary adjustments. Otherwise, it's just bad ethics.<br /><br />I can't believe I'm finally over with "1Q84". Oh, so many options to choose from now! (Sighs)</span>
  <a data-text-id="1563612138853563483" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

//<![CDATA[  

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('8xS9y6Uoy8IbckULR1OUPjtRRlLYe3qvP7x0ey9Nm4Bf2rd9aHMDel+Ft1E/SEOZxRt678qgtTF9jV24clSlGA==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('cfT648yf25bzXCAR7ouVgnbl5lRi7wkFX2pTeVNOlO7dOvBVAcQTLrer0kuWkEIliK/a6XA0xpsdW3q6Dleqdg==')})
  }
//]]>
</script>

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer9153736819345975417"><b>Murakami</b> Haruki (Japanese: <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/11882216' rel='nofollow noopener'>村上 春樹</a>) is a popular contemporary Japanese writer and translator. His work has been described as <i>'easily accessible, yet profoundly complex'</i>. He can be located on Facebook at: <a target='_blank' rel='noopener nofollow' href='https://www.facebook.com/harukimurakamiauthor'>https://www.facebook.com/harukimuraka...</a><br/><br/>Since childhood, Murakami has been heavily influenced by Western culture, particularly Western music and literature. He grew up reading a range of works by Am</span>
  <span id="freeText9153736819345975417" style="display:none"><b>Murakami</b> Haruki (Japanese: <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/11882216" rel="nofollow noopener">村上 春樹</a>) is a popular contemporary Japanese writer and translator. His work has been described as <i>'easily accessible, yet profoundly complex'</i>. He can be located on Facebook at: <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.facebook.com/harukimurakamiauthor">https://www.facebook.com/harukimuraka...</a><br /><br />Since childhood, Murakami has been heavily influenced by Western culture, particularly Western music and literature. He grew up reading a range of works by American writers, such as Kurt Vonnegut and Richard Brautigan, and he is often distinguished from other Japanese writers by his Western influences.<br /><br />Murakami studied drama at Waseda University in Tokyo, where he met his wife, Yoko. His first job was at a record store, which is where one of his main characters, Toru Watanabe in <i>Norwegian Wood</i>, works. Shortly before finishing his studies, Murakami opened the coffeehouse <i>'Peter Cat'</i> which was a jazz bar in the evening in Kokubunji, Tokyo with his wife.<br /><br />Many of his novels have themes and titles that invoke classical music, such as the three books making up <i>The Wind-Up Bird Chronicle</i>: <i>The Thieving Magpie</i> (after Rossini's opera), <i>Bird as Prophet</i> (after a piano piece by Robert Schumann usually known in English as <i>The Prophet Bird</i>), and <i>The Bird-Catcher</i> (a character in Mozart's opera <i>The Magic Flute</i>). Some of his novels take their titles from songs: <i>Dance, Dance, Dance</i> (after The Dells' song, although it is widely thought it was titled after the <i>Beach Boys</i> tune), <i>Norwegian Wood</i> (after The Beatles' song) and <i>South of the Border, West of the Sun</i> (the first part being the title of a song by Nat King Cole).<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="9153736819345975417" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>
